<div class="review nosyndicate" id="review_159257545" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/159257545" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/841949-tracey" title="Tracey"><img alt="Tracey" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1325394892p2/841949.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/159257545?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Feb 23, 2015</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/841949-tracey" title="Tracey">Tracey</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_1" title="did not like it">1 of 5 stars</a>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/841949-tracey?shelf=no-longer-owned">no-longer-owned</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/841949-tracey?shelf=1-star">1-star</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer159257545" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer17704565270848386540">I didn't realize I never bothered with a review for this; I'm cranky today, so now seems like a good time. <br><br>The only good thing about having read this (probably ten years ago) was that I did not pay for it. The woman I was working for at the time forced me to read it; she had read it and thought it was <em>won</em>derful, and since I had some kind of reputation as a reader she wanted (demanded, actually) my opinion. <br><br>I gave it to her. <br><br>I don't really think that has anything to do with the fact that I didn't</br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<span id="freeText17704565270848386540" style="display:none">I didn't realize I never bothered with a review for this; I'm cranky today, so now seems like a good time. <br><br>The only good thing about having read this (probably ten years ago) was that I did not pay for it. The woman I was working for at the time forced me to read it; she had read it and thought it was <em>won</em>derful, and since I had some kind of reputation as a reader she wanted (demanded, actually) my opinion. <br><br>I gave it to her. <br><br>I don't really think that has anything to do with the fact that I didn't work for her much longer. <br><br>I really did hate this book. I hated this book before I knew it was cool to hate it. <br><br>The writing was mediocre – that's not why I hated it, though it made (makes) me sick that something that led the best-seller list for approximately a hundred and sixty-three years was so <em>bad</em>. <br><br>I don't generally go for international suspense thriller type things – that's not why I hated it either. <br><br>I was raised Catholic, and this was an absurd view of a conspiracy-laden church that was so dumb as to be offensive – but that – the Vatican's opinion aside – is still  not why I hated it. <br><br>The reason I got that blue-laser-beam duck-and-cover light in my eyes when I read this was that it completely and totally screwed with art history. I kick myself once in a while that I deleted the web page I put up at the time outlining and illustrating the things Dan Brown simply got wrong about the paintings and artists he pretended to know so much about. There was a lot of stuff up there. I'm no expert; I went to art school, though, and the best teacher I ever had in any subject happened to be the one who taught History of Western Art. It offended me on her behalf that there was so much garbage spewed out in this book. <br><br>Someone defensively said to me once that at least it was bringing attention to Da Vinci and art and the Louvre and … stuff. To me that's like saying an A-list celebrity benefits from the attention of paparazzi. There's a strong similarity: if all a person goes by is the sordid and erroneous crap spoon-fed to them by such authorities as Dan Brown or the National Inquirer (Enquirer? Can't be bothered to check), they would be better off remaining completely ignorant.  <br><br>I'm still cranky. Hm … Did I ever review Twilight …? </br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="17704565270848386540" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>

<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/382712579" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11511116-marwa-a" title="Marwa A."><img alt="Marwa A." src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1408532156p2/11511116.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/382712579?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Nov 04, 2012</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/11511116-marwa-a" title="Marwa A.">Marwa A.</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>
<div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
<span class="uitext reviewText">Open-minded people</span></div>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11511116-marwa-a?shelf=favorite">favorite</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11511116-marwa-a?shelf=thriller-mystery-suspense">thriller-mystery-suspense</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11511116-marwa-a?shelf=religion">religion</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11511116-marwa-a?shelf=baby-i-am-addicted">baby-i-am-addicted</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11511116-marwa-a?shelf=turned-into-a-movie-or-a-tv-show">turned-into-a-movie-or-a-tv-show</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11511116-marwa-a?shelf=goodreads-hype">goodreads-hype</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer382712579" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer8197714994484238574"><strong>
<em>“Life is filled with secrets. You can't learn them all at once.”</em>
</strong><br><br>This is one of the best and most amazing novels I've ever read!<br>When I finished it at almost 3 am, I couldn't sleep.<br>From the instant the book starts, Dan Brown immediately grabs the readers attention, grabbing them by the throat and making them read on right until the end!<br>I like conspiracy theories, so the whole basis of the book was interesting.<br>Obviously most of what is in the book is fiction, but Brown's story telling makes you th</br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<span id="freeText8197714994484238574" style="display:none"><strong>
<em>“Life is filled with secrets. You can't learn them all at once.”</em>
</strong><br><br>This is one of the best and most amazing novels I've ever read!<br>When I finished it at almost 3 am, I couldn't sleep.<br>From the instant the book starts, Dan Brown immediately grabs the readers attention, grabbing them by the throat and making them read on right until the end!<br>I like conspiracy theories, so the whole basis of the book was interesting.<br>Obviously most of what is in the book is fiction, but Brown's story telling makes you think it's real. <br>I thought his use of real places, people and events in what is a fictional story was very clever.<br><br>If you're into mystery/suspense genre, history or secret societies, I recommend this.<br><br>If you are a devout Christian, I don't think I'd recommend this book.</br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="8197714994484238574" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div class="review" id="review_75566740" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/75566740" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1713956-manny" title="Manny"><img alt="Manny" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1227275844p2/1713956.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/75566740?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Oct 24, 2009</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/1713956-manny" title="Manny">Manny</a>

          marked it as to-read




        

    </div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer75566740" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer1979716528870800681">Fellow Goodreaders, I have a confession to make. (Strikes Abe Lincoln pose). No, I haven't actually read it, if that's what you're thinking. But, in a way, it's worse. The fact is, I... er... I... I'm sorry, this is rather difficult for me... I once, ah, I once wrote a letter to a national newspaper supporting Dan Brown's book. And had it published. <br><br>OK, I've said it, and now I feel better. (Wipes sweat from forehead). I tried to find the offending item just now on Google, but it looks as though</br></br></span>
<span id="freeText1979716528870800681" style="display:none">Fellow Goodreaders, I have a confession to make. (Strikes Abe Lincoln pose). No, I haven't actually read it, if that's what you're thinking. But, in a way, it's worse. The fact is, I... er... I... I'm sorry, this is rather difficult for me... I once, ah, I once wrote a letter to a national newspaper supporting Dan Brown's book. And had it published. <br><br>OK, I've said it, and now I feel better. (Wipes sweat from forehead). I tried to find the offending item just now on Google, but it looks as though well-meaning people have done their best to hide the evidence. I'd really like to thank them for that. Anyway, if you search on my name and "Da Vinci Code" or "Dan Brown", you'll find pointers to it, though I've so far been unable to retrieve the actual text.<br><br>As far as I can recall, the background was roughly as follows. A columnist in the <em>Independent</em>, Christina Patterson, had written an article in which she dismissively attacked <em>The Da Vinci Code</em>, and cited a recent interview with the author. Mr Brown had been asked why he thought his book was a success, and had said something about how he believed that what people liked was books with "puzzles and treasure hunts". Patterson remarked with evident contempt that there were no puzzles and treasure hunts in, if I'm remembering correctly, Shakespeare, Dickens or Tolstoy.<br><br>Well... I'm a big fan of due process. I thought Saddam Hussein was a monster who deserved death fifty times over; but I opposed the Iraq War on the grounds that virtually the only bad thing he hadn't done was to harbor secret weapons of mass destruction, which was the ostensible reason for invading his country. My feelings about Dan Brown were similar. So the letter pointed out that Ms. Patterson was just cherry-picking her authors. As far as I was aware, she was quite correct in saying that Shakespeare, Dickens and Tolstoy didn't do puzzles and treasure-hunts; but if she'd wanted to argue the contrary position, she could just as easily have cited Lewis Carroll, James Joyce and Vladimir Nabokov. The real problem with Dan Brown was not the subject matter, but the quality of the writing.<br><br>Sigh. I thought I'd better come clean. I'd rather that you hear it from me directly than, you know, just stumble over it by accident when you're following a random link. Maybe, some day, you'll be able to forgive me. And while we're talking about Dan Brown and random links, check <a href="http://houseoffame.blogspot.com/2006/05/cipher-of-leonardo.html" rel="nofollow">this</a> out. It's much funnier than my so-called review.<br/></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="1979716528870800681" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>

<div class="review" id="review_846566" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/846566" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26737-mary" title="mary"><img alt="mary" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1174367314p2/26737.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/846566?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Apr 23, 2007</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/26737-mary" title="mary">mary</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_3" title="liked it">3 of 5 stars</a>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer846566" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer7224421709447316397">it's an action movie! it's a book! it's an action movie!<br><br>it surprises me that the movie version was so dull, having such a simple adapted screenplay to write. this book reads like a blockbuster looks. and i will admit that i totally loved it while i was reading and forgot it promptly after, but i did the same when i watched vin diesel in XXX. <br><br>it's a very fun read. it's horrendously written, the characters are rather shallow, there are enough chase scenes and things popping out of dark corners to</br></br></br></br></span>
<span id="freeText7224421709447316397" style="display:none">it's an action movie! it's a book! it's an action movie!<br><br>it surprises me that the movie version was so dull, having such a simple adapted screenplay to write. this book reads like a blockbuster looks. and i will admit that i totally loved it while i was reading and forgot it promptly after, but i did the same when i watched vin diesel in XXX. <br><br>it's a very fun read. it's horrendously written, the characters are rather shallow, there are enough chase scenes and things popping out of dark corners to satisfy anyone's juvenile appetite for suspense. and if you're catholic or knowledgeable at all about religion, it does provide some fodder for thought in between all the drama. but after you think, you realize that none of this information, true or not, is really that shocking and has little effect on the catholic faith. people would do well to learn about the nature and history of their belief system a little more.  <br><br>i still can't figure out how this book has caused so much controversy. let me rephrase that. i still can't figure out how people can be so oblivious and closeminded as to be scandalized by drivelly fiction. i was on an airplane a while ago and i sat next to a man reading "cracking the davinci code" or one of those other pissed off books that came out soon after. i asked him what he thought of the book. he said, 'oh! i've never read the da vinci code! it goes against the catholic church! why would i read such blasphemy? i just want to be armed with information when i speak to some simple-minded person who believes that heretic dan brown!" <br><br>i wish i could make that kind of stuff up. <br><br>i was silent, smiled and nodded, and quickly opened up Kavalier and Clay. i simply had no idea how to respond to someone who was taking notes on a book to collect information on a novel that he had never read so he could disprove the opinions of those who thought it was true. what?<br><br><br/></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="7224421709447316397" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>

<div class="review nosyndicate" id="review_2917483" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2917483" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/182835-crystal" title="Crystal"><img alt="Crystal" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1271797645p2/182835.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2917483?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Aug 24, 2007</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/182835-crystal" title="Crystal">Crystal</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_1" title="did not like it">1 of 5 stars</a>
<div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
<span class="uitext reviewText">men, conspiracy buffs, religious skeptics</span></div>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/182835-crystal?shelf=books-i-didn-t-enjoy">books-i-didn-t-enjoy</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/182835-crystal?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer2917483" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer4121378118617486690">Believe it or not, my BOSS lent me this book. He said it was so great that I HAD to read it, and he pressed it into my hands. I said thanks in surprise, because that was about as personal as my boss ever got.  well, imagine the awkwardness when I didn't really like the book. what do you say to the man who signs your paychecks when you think his book is nothing more than a creative version of what I call the man's romance novel--boring, average man (okay, in this book very smart--but not special l</span>
<span id="freeText4121378118617486690" style="display:none">Believe it or not, my BOSS lent me this book. He said it was so great that I HAD to read it, and he pressed it into my hands. I said thanks in surprise, because that was about as personal as my boss ever got.  well, imagine the awkwardness when I didn't really like the book. what do you say to the man who signs your paychecks when you think his book is nothing more than a creative version of what I call the man's romance novel--boring, average man (okay, in this book very smart--but not special looking) finds incredibly beautiful and often smart woman to follow him through thick and thin and all manner of ludicrous plots and danger for some unknown reason.  It's just a religious conspiracy version of the Bourne Identity or any other book like that.  What average guy wouldn't love to believe that a hot woman would follow him around, as he impresses and saves her from all kinds of dangers.<br>except I don't get much out of that myself, not being a guy, and I didn't think there was much to the whole plot of religious conspiracy, having majored in Biblical studies in college, and knowing how hokey and unrealistic some of this stuff is. and don't even get me started on weak arguments and faulty logic. (SPOILER ALERT: "don't worship Jesus because He was a normal guy who married a woman and had a kid--no, but go ahead and pray to his fully human wife who's DEAD!" yes, clearly a good argument.) <br>but, hey, my boss likes Jennifer Aniston a lot too, and she doesn't do much for me either. ;)</br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="4121378118617486690" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>

<div class="section">
<div class="review nosyndicate" id="review_26773923" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/26773923" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/972904-jake" title="Jake"><img alt="Jake" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1230756051p2/972904.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/26773923?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Jul 09, 2008</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/972904-jake" title="Jake">Jake</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_1" title="did not like it">1 of 5 stars</a>
<span class="greyText"> · </span>
<a class="lightGreyText" href="/book/show/85266.The_Da_Vinci_Code" title="Hardcover 9780385504201">review of another edition</a>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/972904-jake?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/972904-jake?shelf=pulp">pulp</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer26773923" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer15439235659606800084">If for some reason you haven’t heard of the Da Vinci Code before now, I have no help for you. I live under a fairly substantive rock, and even I had heard of this book to death. I had even attempted to read it once or twice, during my stint at Waldenbooks, but couldn’t really get into it. So I gave up, and decided to relegate it to the same place as Titanic and Forrest Gump—works of popular culture that I have no interest in, and therefore, will not partake of. But in a testament to the power of</span>
<span id="freeText15439235659606800084" style="display:none">If for some reason you haven’t heard of the Da Vinci Code before now, I have no help for you. I live under a fairly substantive rock, and even I had heard of this book to death. I had even attempted to read it once or twice, during my stint at Waldenbooks, but couldn’t really get into it. So I gave up, and decided to relegate it to the same place as Titanic and Forrest Gump—works of popular culture that I have no interest in, and therefore, will not partake of. But in a testament to the power of the feminine wile, SV convinced me to read it, and so, at the suggestion of Professor Mortis, borrowed a copy from my local library before setting out for the Thanksgiving holiday (Thanks to PG and Laurion for their lending offers, though I did not take them up on it.).<br><br>So what did I think of this incredibly popular, highly controversial work that sold literally millions of copies, spawned a movie, and produced comments from the Vatican itself? In a word, it’s bad. Other appropriate single words might be awful, atrocious, terrible, or abominable. Other similar synonyms can be found here.<br><br>The Da Vinci Code is a combination of bad writing, shoddy history, poor plotting, and unconvincing characters that blends together to form a barely readable work. The fact that anyone took this work seriously in any capacity is amazing to me. The characters are flat, boring, and none of them exceed two dimensions at best. The “history” is so riddled with holes as to be completely unbelievable to the semi-alert observer (my favorite part is the explanation of how the Church’s desire to destroy the “sacred feminine” is responsible for Orthodox Jews and devout Muslims barring women from certain rites.). And finally, the plot just makes no damn sense. Even the much vaunted puzzles are fairly trite, and usually explained so quickly that it’s barely worth thinking about. The final puzzle that the main character is faced with is so mind-bogglingly obvious that anyone who knows how to draw a Mogen David (Star of David) can figure it out instantly.<br><br>So the question for me became this: given how awful this book is, why the hell is it so popular? I can offer the following explanations/observations.<br><br>First off, Brown employs a clever (and I use the term loosely) narrative structure in which he ends every single chapter on a cliffhanger. This forces the reader to continue reading, hoping to reach a payoff or stopping point that is never quite realized. Thus the reader keeps going to the next chapter, hoping to finally hit a point where the story will relax, only to discover that the story never really relaxes. It just plods relentlessly forward, like the Terminator chasing Sarah Connor with a SuDoKu puzzle.<br><br>In a similar vein, Brown keeps his chapters short. In fact, I’ve read verse poetry that was longer than some of the chapters in this book (and considerably better written). This helps the reader feel as though they are making progress, because they’re suddenly on Chapter 44, and can reach Chapter 50 with only a few extra minutes of reading. Hooray for the short attention span.<br><br>Likewise, Brown taps into some popular political and historical fallacies that are guaranteed to make him well-liked. Namely that the Catholic Church is an evil conspiracy (Christianity is one of the few religions it’s still ok to hate in this day and age, after all) and the notion that ancient Europeans were all ecologically conscious, goddess-worshipping pacifists until the evil patriarchy destroyed their edenic culture. And hey, both of those are popular, if totally un-nuanced and historically questionable viewpoints, but shoddy history is always popular with the masses. The popularity of this book is proof enough.<br><br>Knowing that I’m one of the last people in the United States to have read this book, I can hardly imagine my recommendation can save anyone at this point, but on the off hand chance it can—don’t read this book. It’s a waste of time. At the very least, take it out of the library so you don’t put more money into the pocket of an author of questionable talent. There’s much better writers in this world.</br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="15439235659606800084" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>

<div class="review nosyndicate" id="review_75277491" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/75277491" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2863914-joel" title="Joel"><img alt="Joel" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1407175214p2/2863914.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/75277491?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">Jun 30, 2011</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/2863914-joel" title="Joel">Joel</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_1" title="did not like it">1 of 5 stars</a>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2863914-joel?shelf=to-not-read-ever">to-not-read-ever</a>, 
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2863914-joel?shelf=30-day-book-challenge">30-day-book-challenge</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer75277491" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer15048448130454976298">One night about five years back, I stopped over at a friend's house to drop off a movie I'd borrowed. Her husband greeted me at the door and said she would have come out to say hi, but she was too busy reading. "Oh, what's she reading?" I asked. "The Da Vinci Code. She said it's, like, the best book ever."<br><br>I was shocked and appalled. Surely not! Not from my 19th century lit-loving friend, whose main criterion for a good book is that the author is no longer living, and has preferably been that way</br></br></span>
<span id="freeText15048448130454976298" style="display:none">One night about five years back, I stopped over at a friend's house to drop off a movie I'd borrowed. Her husband greeted me at the door and said she would have come out to say hi, but she was too busy reading. "Oh, what's she reading?" I asked. "The Da Vinci Code. She said it's, like, the best book ever."<br><br>I was shocked and appalled. Surely not! Not from my 19th century lit-loving friend, whose main criterion for a good book is that the author is no longer living, and has preferably been that way for at least 100 years. I talked to her on the phone the next day and she explained, to my relief, that she didn't think the book was <em>well written</em> (you know, <em>good</em>), she just thought it was interesting, and appreciated the way it subverted religion and revealed the lengths people will go to to justify and reinforce their beliefs, even in the face of overwhelming evidence to the contrary (she was in a big anti-religion phase at the time).<br><br>"Phew!" I thought. "Friendship crisis averted."<br><br>Then just today at work, conversation turned to popular fiction. It started with Stieg Larsson, and that I am totally on board with -- even though the man doesn't so much write stories as explain to you what is happening in them, in Lisbeth Salander he created a character for the ages (hyperbole alert). I kept my scoffs to myself when co-worker A mentioned that she had <em>looved</em> Water for Elephants, finding it a wonderful break from her usual diet of <em>James Patterson</em>, though my nose may have begun to point ever so slightly upwards (full disclosure: I thought the former a perfectly cromulent, perfectly forgettable plane read, though I can't recall ever reading The World's Best-Selling Author).<br><br>But then.<br><br>Then co-worker B said it: "Have you read The Da Vinci Code? That is seriously the <em>best</em> book ever."<br><br>I could have filled a terabyte hard drive with my sighs and eye rolls. <br><br>Um. Except. Except I have never <em>read</em> The Da Vinci Code, or anything by Dan Brown. Normally I am strongly against literary snobbery (you have to be when you read as many genre books as I have been lately), but everyone has a weak spot, and mine is that book. Can't say why. The hype? The overblown controversy? The terrible movie? Tom Hanks' terrible, terrible mullet?<br><br><img class="escapedImg" src="https://p.gr-assets.com/540x540/fit/hostedimages/1380328445/691663.jpg"><br><br>The thing is, sometimes I will disparage this book aloud, in conversation, as if I know what I am talking about. Look, I even gave it a star rating. I am such a pretentious jerk sometimes.<br><br>But I can't help feeling that the book deserves it.<br><br><em>Facebook 30 Day Book Challenge Day 23: Book you tell people you’ve read, but haven’t.</em></br></br></br></br></br></br></img></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="15048448130454976298" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>

<div class="section">
<div class="review" id="review_18611979" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
<link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/18611979" itemprop="url"/>
<a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1022982-brad" title="Brad"><img alt="Brad" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1308747432p2/1022982.jpg"/></a>
<div class="left bodycol">
<div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
<a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/18611979?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">May 02, 2009</a>
<a class="user" href="/user/show/1022982-brad" title="Brad">Brad</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_1" title="did not like it">1 of 5 stars</a>
<div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=most-hated">most-hated</a>
</div>
</div>
<div class="reviewText stacked">
<span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer18611979" style="">
<span id="freeTextContainer9095106948139536699">I freely admit that my disdain for <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968.The_Da_Vinci_Code" title="The Da Vinci Code by Dan Brown">The Da Vinci Code</a> is my own personal backlash over its popularity. <br><br>Dan Brown isn't a terrible writer, despite facing that charge from many experienced readers. He has a likable style, and he drives the pace of the book relentlessly, which is exactly what one would want from a pulpy adventure that one can take to the beach. <br><br>Likewise, the charge that <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968.The_Da_Vinci_Code" title="The Da Vinci Code by Dan Brown">The Da Vinci Code</a> is somehow a failure because it is in any way inaccurate or unbelievable is unfair. The story is</br></br></br></br></span>
<span id="freeText9095106948139536699" style="display:none">I freely admit that my disdain for <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968.The_Da_Vinci_Code" title="The Da Vinci Code by Dan Brown">The Da Vinci Code</a> is my own personal backlash over its popularity. <br><br>Dan Brown isn't a terrible writer, despite facing that charge from many experienced readers. He has a likable style, and he drives the pace of the book relentlessly, which is exactly what one would want from a pulpy adventure that one can take to the beach. <br><br>Likewise, the charge that <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968.The_Da_Vinci_Code" title="The Da Vinci Code by Dan Brown">The Da Vinci Code</a> is somehow a failure because it is in any way inaccurate or unbelievable is unfair. The story is <em>fiction</em>, after all, and one should expect to have his/her credulity stretched, especially when reading pulp that is written with the screen in mind (as <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968.The_Da_Vinci_Code" title="The Da Vinci Code by Dan Brown">The Da Vinci Code</a> surely was).<br><br>I even enjoyed the Sunday afternoon it took me to read <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968.The_Da_Vinci_Code" title="The Da Vinci Code by Dan Brown">The Da Vinci Code</a>. It was an absolute waste of time and exactly what I wanted to be doing, sitting on a comfy sofa, drinking tea and reading about self-flagellating albino monks (and other fun things).<br><br>I've given many books that are just as good as <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968.The_Da_Vinci_Code" title="The Da Vinci Code by Dan Brown">The Da Vinci Code</a> and even some that are worse three stars, and I meant every star. The truth is that on its own merits, I'd have given <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968.The_Da_Vinci_Code" title="The Da Vinci Code by Dan Brown">The Da Vinci Code</a> a similar rating if not for a repeated experience that led to my backlash.<br><br>At the beginning of every semester, in a bid to get to know my students better, I play a memory game wherein the students provide me with their favourite things (books, food, music) and some personal details (people they hate, people they love, things they are proud of), then I connect something about them, something that stands out for me, with their name. It is a good start in getting to know the students, but it has also led to my hatred for Dan Brown's <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968.The_Da_Vinci_Code" title="The Da Vinci Code by Dan Brown">The Da Vinci Code</a>.<br><br>A good half of the students that enter my courses declare that they don't have favourite books, and/or they've only ever read three books in their lives -- two involuntary (both assigned by an English teacher, and always seeming to include <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/988811.To_Kill_a_Mockingbird" title="To Kill a Mockingbird by Harper Lee">To Kill a Mockingbird</a>) and one voluntary (<a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968.The_Da_Vinci_Code" title="The Da Vinci Code by Dan Brown">The Da Vinci Code</a>). What pisses me off most is that even if these people liked <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968.The_Da_Vinci_Code" title="The Da Vinci Code by Dan Brown">The Da Vinci Code</a>, Brown's novel didn't spur them on to read more. They read the <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968.The_Da_Vinci_Code" title="The Da Vinci Code by Dan Brown">The Da Vinci Code</a>, enjoyed it or didn't, then went back to their reading apathy. <br><br>Moreover, if I could convince people to read one book voluntarily, one book for their pleasure, it would not be ANY cheesy, pulpy, low grade adventure story. It's like pouring a glass of $9 dollar wine for a person who is trying wine for the first time. They may enjoy the glass, but they're not going to choose wine as their alcohol of choice based on Fortant de France. <br><br>And for that reason, I hate <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968.The_Da_Vinci_Code" title="The Da Vinci Code by Dan Brown">The Da Vinci Code</a>. It is the cheap wine that keeps people away from the joy of good wine, and while I admit that it is the fault of popular culture rather than <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/author/show/630.Dan_Brown" title="Dan Brown">Dan Brown</a>, each reader I find who stops at <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968.The_Da_Vinci_Code" title="The Da Vinci Code by Dan Brown">The Da Vinci Code</a> makes me hate the book a little bit more.</br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="9095106948139536699" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</span>

<div class="brown_comment" id="comment_9542566">
<div class="xhr_comment_photo">
<a alt="Richard" href="/user/show/1662632-richard"><img alt="1662632" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1402512503p1/1662632.jpg"/></a>
</div>
<div class="xhr_comment_body">
<a href="/user/show/1662632-richard" style="font-weight:bold">Richard</a>
<span id="freeTextContainer12688757564157321056">I read <em>Da Vinci Code</em> and enjoyed it, but it was a mildly guilty pleasure. The analogy with junk food is mostly appropriate, at least for readers. (No</span>
<span id="freeText12688757564157321056" style="display:none">I read <em>Da Vinci Code</em> and enjoyed it, but it was a mildly guilty pleasure. The analogy with junk food is mostly appropriate, at least for readers. (No one <em>must</em> read, but it works for those of us that choose to read.)<br><br>Every hour I spend reading fluff is an hour not spent reading something I am somewhat sure is "better for me" in some way. But what I think is better for me has no certain relationship to anyone else's self-improvement. <br><br>That said, it does grate when someone does a mediocre job and is lauded with wealth and popular acclaim. Dan Brown isn't bad, but there are plenty of authors I'd rather see receiving bucketfuls of money. I'm much happier with the success of J.K. Rowling, for example.<br><br><br>And Trevor— please do read both <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/119073.The_Name_of_the_Rose" title="The Name of the Rose by Umberto Eco">The Name of the Rose</a> and <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968.The_Da_Vinci_Code" title="The Da Vinci Code by Dan Brown">The Da Vinci Code</a>. The former for yourself (it is a <em>splendid</em> novel), and the latter so we can all greedily consume your review.<br><br>Hmmm, perhaps we should start a series of polls wherein we can vote on what Trevor should read and review next?</br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="12688757564157321056" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
<br/>

      23. September, 18:51 Uhr

    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

Nicholas dare<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText16127964708172089247\" style=\"display:none\">Ekaterinburg, Russia: July 16, 1918. Ten months have passed since Nicholas II’s reign was cut short by revolutionaries. Tonight, the White Army advances on the town where the Tsar and his family are being held captive by the Bolsheviks. Nicholas dares to hope for salvation. Instead, the Romanovs are coldly and methodically executed.Moscow: Present Day. Atlanta lawyer Miles Lord, fluent in Russian and well versed in the country’s history, is thrilled to be in Moscow on the eve of such a momentous event. After the fall of Communism and a succession of weak governments, the Russian people have voted to bring back the monarchy. The new tsar will be chosen from the distant relatives of Nicholas II by a specially appointed commission, and Miles’ job is to perform a background check on the Tsarist candidate favored by a powerful group of Western businessmen. But research quickly becomes the least of Miles’ concerns when he is nearly killed by gunmen on a city plaza.Suddenly Miles is racing across continents, shadowed by nefarious henchmen. At first, his only question is why people are pursuing him. But after a strange conversation with a mysterious Russian, who steers Miles toward the writings of Rasputin, he becomes desperate to know more–most important, what really happened to the family of Russia’s last tsar?His only companion is Akilina Petrov, a Russian circus performer sympathetic to his struggle, and his only guide is a cryptic message from Rasputin that implies that the bloody night of so long ago is not the last chapter in the Romanovs’ story . . . and that someone might even have survived the massacre. The prophecy’s implications are earth-shattering–not only for the future of the tsar and mother Russia, but also for Miles himself.Steve Berry, national bestselling author of the phenomenal thriller The Amber Room, once again delves into rich historical fact to produce an explosive page-turner. In The Romanov Prophecy, the authentic and the speculative meld into a fascinating and exceptionally suspenseful work of fiction.From the Hardcover edition.<\/span>\n  <a <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/630.Dan_Brown">About Dan Brown</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
<a href="/author/show/630.Dan_Brown"><img alt="630" class="right" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1399396714p2/630.jpg"/></a>
<div class="readable">
<span id="freeTextContainer8730780192876993794">Dan Brown is the author of numerous #1 bestselling novels, including <em>The Da Vinci Code</em>, which has become one of the best selling novels of all time as well as the subject of intellectual debate among readers and scholars. Brown’s novels are published in 52 languages around the world with 200 million copies in print.<br><br>In 2005, Brown was named one of the 100 Most Influential People in the World by TIM</br></br></span>
<span id="freeText8730780192876993794" style="display:none">Dan Brown is the author of numerous #1 bestselling novels, including <em>The Da Vinci Code</em>, which has become one of the best selling novels of all time as well as the subject of intellectual debate among readers and scholars. Brown’s novels are published in 52 languages around the world with 200 million copies in print.<br><br>In 2005, Brown was named one of the 100 Most Influential People in the World by TIME Magazine, whose editors credited him with “keeping the publishing industry afloat; renewed interest in Leonardo da Vinci and early Christian history; spiking tourism to Paris and Rome; a growing membership in secret societies; the ire of Cardinals in Rome; eight books denying the claims of the novel and seven guides to read along with it; a flood of historical thrillers; and a major motion picture franchise.”<br><br>The son of a mathematics teacher and a church organist, Brown was raised on a prep school campus where he developed a fascination with the paradoxical interplay between science and religion. These themes eventually formed the backdrop for his books. He is a graduate of Amherst College and Phillips Exeter Academy, where he later returned to teach English before focusing his attention full time to writing.<br><br>Brown is currently at work on a new book as well as the Columbia Pictures film version of his most recent novel.<br/></br></br></br></br></br></br></span>
<a data-text-id="8730780192876993794" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
</div>
